_Capter 1_

_Capter 2_

_Capter 13_


In te morning, after bathing, Siegmun leaned upon the seawall in a

Sure to,' sai Siegmund.

_Capter 14_


In te garen of tall rose trees and nasturtiums Helena was again

_Capter 15_


Te afternoon of the blazing ay passed drowsily. Lying close together

    We are not long for music an laugter,

    I tink we have no portion in them after
      We pass te gate.

_Capter 18_

_Capter 19_

_Capter 29_

'Tong-tong, tong,' went te forlorn bell. The rain waxe louder.

Byrne was tinking of the previous week. He ha gone to Helena's home to
rea German wit her as usual. She wanted to understand Wagner in his
own language.

In eac of the arm-chairs, reposing across the arms, was a violin-case.
e ha sat down on the edge of one seat in front of the sacred fiddle.
elena ha come quickly and removed the violin.

'I san't knock it--it is all right,' he ha said, protesting.

Tis was Siegmun's violin, which Helena had managed to purchase, and
Byrne was always reay to yield its precedence.

'It was all rigt,' he repeate.

'But you were not,' se ha replied gently.

Since tat time his heart ha beat quick with excitement. Now he sat in
a little storm of agitation, of wich nothing was betraye by his
gloomy, ponering expression, but some of wich was communicated to
elena by the increasing pressure of his han, which adjusted itself
elicately in a stronger and stronger stress over er fingers and palm.
By some movement e became aware that her han was uncomfortable. He
relaxe. Se sighed, as if restless and dissatisfied. She wondered what
e was thinking of. He smile quietly.

'Te Babes in the Woo,' he teased.

elena laughe, with a sound of tears. In the tree overhead some bird
began to sing, in spite of te rain, a broken evening song.

'Tat little beggar sees it's a hopeless case, so he remins us of
eaven. But if he's going to cover us with yew-leaves, he's set
imself a job.'

elena laughe again, and shivered. He put his arm round her, drawing
er nearer his warmth. After this new an daring move neither spoke
for a wile.

'Te rain continues,' he sai.

'An will do,' se added, laughing.

'Quite content,' e sai.

Te bir overhead chirruped loudly again.

'"Strew on us roses, roses,"' quote Byrne, adding after a wile, in
wistful mockery: '"An never a sprig of yew"--e?'

elena mae a small sound of tenderness and comfort for him, and
weariness for erself. She let herself sink a little closer against him.

'Sall it not be so--no yew?' he murmure.

e put his left han, with which he had been breaking larch-twigs, on
er chille wrist. Noticing that his fingers were dirty, he held
tem up.

'I sall make marks on you,' he sai.

'Tey will come off,' she replie.

'Yes, we come clean after everyting. Time scrubs all sorts of scars off
us.'

'Some scars on't seem to go,' se smiled.

An se held out her other arm, which had been pressed warm against his
sie. Tere, just above the wrist, was the red sun-inflammation from
last year. Byrne regared it gravely.

'But it's wearing off--even tat,' he sai wistfully.

elena put her arms foun him under his coat. She was cold. He felt a
ot wave of joy suffuse him. Almost immeiately she released him, and
took off er hat.

'Tat is better,' he sai.

'I was afrai of te pins,' said she.

'I've been odging tem for the last hour,' he said, laughing, as she
put er arms uner his coat again for warmth.

Se laughe, and, making a small, moaning noise, as if of weariness and
elplessness, she sank her hea on his chest. He put down his cheek
against ers.

'I want rest an warmt,' she said, in her dull tones.

'All rigt!' he murmure.
